The verb from which this term is derived, however, 好く (suku), more closely resembles the verbal usage of like in English.
良い	よい	良い	<div class="part-of-speech">Adjective</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">good</li></ul>	jo̞i	<span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>よ</span>い	<ruby>良<rt><span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>よ</span></rt></ruby>い	Most often written in hiragana. いい	いい		<div class="part-of-speech">Adjective</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">(colloquial, informal) good</li><li class="definition-entry">(colloquial, predicative only) enough, not wanted any more<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><ruby>酒<rt>さけ</rt></ruby>はもう<b>いい</b></li><li class="romanji-example">sake wa mō <b>ii</b></li><li class="english-example">I have had <b>enough</b> wine.I <b>don’t want any more</b> wine.</li></ul></li><li class="definition-entry">(ても~, て~) Indicates permission; can, may</li></ul>	iː	<span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>い</span>い		Most often written in this form. The kanji spelling 良い is generally reserved for more formal writing.<br>This form does not inflect — it is only used in the 終止形 (shūshikei, “terminal form”) and 連体形 (rentaikei, “attributive form”) of ii. All other grammatical forms are based on 良い (yoi). Compare the 連用形 (ren’yōkei, “continuative or adverbial form”) yoku or the 過去形 (kakokei, “past tense”) yokatta; there is no *iku or *ikatta form.<br>In ironic usages denoting “very bad” or “embarrassing”, this form is preferred to the yoi form.

遊ぶ	あそぶ	遊ぶ	<div class="part-of-speech">Verb</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">play, amuse oneself, enjoy oneself</li><li class="definition-entry">spend one's time idle, do nothing</li><li class="definition-entry">for an item not to be in use, be spare or idle</li></ul>	a̠so̞bɯ̟	あそぶ	<ruby>遊<rt><span class="normal-rt">あ</span><span class="normal-rt">そ</span></rt></ruby>ぶ	Unlike "play" in English, this term is applied to both children and adults. (This leads to usage errors such as "I enjoy playing with my girlfriend on weekends.") It is used broadly for any enjoyable activity such as socializing.

大きな	おおきな	大きな	<div class="part-of-speech">Adnominal</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">big, large</li></ul>		おおきな	<ruby>大<rt><span class="normal-rt">お</span><span class="normal-rt">お</span></rt></ruby>きなな	This term may only be used to directly modify a noun. In this grammatical role, it may be used largely interchangeably with 大きい (ōkii), the -i adjective form. In predicative use, only 大きい (ōkii) may be used: あれは*大きだ (are wa *ōki da) is incorrect grammar. Also, there is no adverbial form *大きに (*ōki ni), only the regular -ku adverbial form 大きく (ōkiku) of the -i adjective 大きい (ōkii).

卵	たまご	卵	<div class="part-of-speech">Noun</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">an egg, including fish roe</li><li class="definition-entry">the beginning or start of something (compare the uses of English terms kernel and seed)</li><li class="definition-entry">a beginner or rookie at something</li></ul>	ta̠ma̠ɡo̞	た<span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>ま</span>ご	<ruby>卵<rt><span class="normal-rt">た</span><span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>ま</span><span class="normal-rt">ご</span></rt></ruby>	This term in Japanese is nearly as broad in use as English egg, indicating an egg as laid by various oviparous organisms, and an egg as used in cooking. 弟	おとうと	弟	<div class="part-of-speech">Noun</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">one’s own younger brother</li><li class="definition-entry">a younger male</li></ul>	o̞to̞ːto̞	おとう<span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>と</span>	<ruby>弟<rt><span class="normal-rt">お</span><span class="normal-rt">と</span><span class="normal-rt">う</span><span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>と</span></rt></ruby>	Used when referring to one’s own younger brother. To refer to someone else’s younger brother, the suffixed form 弟さん (otōto-san) is used instead. When addressing one’s own younger brother, the given name is used, often with a suffix, such as 太郎君 (Tarō-kun). Contrast with addressing one’s own older brother, when one uses the honorific お兄さん (onīsan).

"<b>Not at all</b>, I should thank you."</li></ul></li></ul>	iːe̞	いい<span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>え</span>		May be considered overly direct. あそこ	あそこ		<div class="part-of-speech">Pronoun</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">(deictically) that place, area, or part over there (far from the speaker and the addressee)<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><ruby>私<rt>わたし</rt></ruby>の<ruby>子<rt>こ</rt></ruby><ruby>供<rt>ども</rt></ruby>は<b>あそこ</b>で<ruby>遊<rt>あそ</rt></ruby>んでいますよ。</li><li class="romanji-example">Watashi no kodomo wa <b>asoko</b> de asonde imasu yo.</li><li class="english-example">My child is playing <b>over there</b>.</li></ul></li><li class="definition-entry">(deictically) that organization or team over there (far from the speaker and the addressee)</li><li class="definition-entry">(anaphorically) that place, area, or part we both know (both the speaker and the addressee know)</li><li class="definition-entry">(anaphorically) that organization or team we both know (both the speaker and the addressee know)</li><li class="definition-entry">(slang, by extension from the sense of “down there”) genitals</li></ul>	a̠so̞ko̞	あそこ		The hiragana spelling あそこ is preferred to the kanji spelling 彼処, which is rarely used.<br>あそこ, as a meaning of place, is mostly followed by some particles with verbs as follows.<br>あそこに: with verbs of movement (go, come, move, transfer, ...) or verbs of existence(be, exist, stay, ...).<br>あそこへ: with verbs of movement (go, come, move, transfer, ...).<br>あそこで: with the other verbs.

It is unclear if this leading /s/ is indicative of an earlier same, or if this was an epenthesis for euphony to avoid vowel clusters, or for other reasons. This /s/ is also seen in 青 (ao, becoming sao in old compounds) and 稲 (ine, becoming shine in old compounds). Given that this /s/ only ever appears interstitially, and given the semantics, this /s/ may be cognate with Korean interfix -ᄉ- (-s-) used to mark possession, much like English -s-.

The -te / -de + iru grammatical form is usually described in English as equivalent to the present progressive.  However, for Japanese instantaneous verbs, this -te / -de + iru grammatical form instead more commonly indicates that the action of the verb has completed, and the result of the verb is the new current state.

タバコ	タバコ		<div class="part-of-speech">Noun</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">tobacco</li></ul>	ta̠ba̠ko̞	タバコ		Unusually for gairaigo (non-Middle Chinese borrowings), tabako is often written in hiragana. This is particularly seen on convenience store signs, indicating that cigarettes are for sale. This usage (hiragana rather than katakana) is attributed to this being an old borrowing, dating to the nanban trade period (16th & 17th century), before the convention of writing gairaigo as katakana had developed.

あの	あの		<div class="part-of-speech">Adnominal</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">(deictically) that ... over there (far from the speaker and the addressee)<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><b>あの</b><ruby>車<rt>くるま</rt></ruby>は<ruby>緑<rt>みどり</rt></ruby>です。</li><li class="romanji-example"><b>Ano</b> kuruma wa midori desu.</li><li class="english-example"><b>That</b> car <b>over there</b> is green.</li></ul></li><li class="definition-entry">(anaphorically) that ... we both know (both the speaker and the addressee know)</li></ul>	a̠no̞	あの		As an adnominal, あの must be followed by a noun or noun phrase.<br>あの is also used to refer to things and experiences that both the speaker and the listener have knowledge of. Compare the two following sentences,<br>あの本はやすいですか？Ano hon wa yasui desu ka?Is that book (over there, which you and I can see) cheap?<br>その本はやすいですか？Sono hon wa yasui desu ka?Is that book (I'm pointing to/near you/of yours) cheap? / Is that book which you (have/saw/were talking about) cheap?<br>The hiragana spelling (あの) is preferred to the kanji spelling (彼の), which is rarely used.

絵	え	絵	<div class="part-of-speech">Noun</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">picture, painting</li><li class="definition-entry">drawing, sketch</li></ul>	e̞	<span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>え</span>	<ruby>絵<rt><span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>え</span></rt></ruby>	The kanji 絵 and 画 (also pronounced e, but can also be pronounced as ga), both having the same meanings and on-readings, can be confusing. While 絵 is used to mean pictures in the "drawn" sense (for example, 挿絵 (sashie), "illustration"), it can also refer to art in front of the speaker, such as 浮世絵 (ukiyoe, “ukiyo-e”) or 蒔絵 (makie, “sprinkled lacquer”). 画 refers to art by extension, for example, 日本画 (nihonga, “Japanese painting”). Sometimes, both kanji can be interchanged, an example being 影絵, 影画 (kagee, “shadow play”)

水	みず	水	<div class="part-of-speech">Noun</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">water (sometimes cold and/or fresh)</li><li class="definition-entry">drinking water</li><li class="definition-entry">a fluid, liquid</li><li class="definition-entry">a flood</li></ul>	mʲizɨ	みず	<ruby>水<rt><span class="normal-rt">み</span><span class="normal-rt">ず</span></rt></ruby>	Note that, while mizu is the most general Japanese term for “water”, it does refer specifically to “water that is not hot”. 家族	かぞく	家族	<div class="part-of-speech">Noun</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">a family, a household</li></ul>	ka̠zo̞kɯ̟	<span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>か</span>ぞく	<ruby>家<rt><span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>か</span></rt></ruby><ruby>族<rt><span class="normal-rt">ぞ</span><span class="normal-rt">く</span></rt></ruby>	家族 refers to one’s own family, particularly immediate family – parents, children, and siblings. To refer to another’s family, one uses the honorific form ご家族 (go-kazoku, “another’s family”, honorific). To refer to one’s relatives generally, one uses 親戚 (shinseki, “relative”), which includes even distant relatives. For first cousins – i.e. aunts, uncles, and close cousins – one may use 近親 (kinshin, “close relative”). For close people, not necessarily family, one may use 身内 (miuchi, “close family, close friends”).

じゃ	じゃ		<div class="part-of-speech">Conjunction</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">(colloquial) so, then—used for connecting to a reasonable conclusion, supposition</li></ul><div class="part-of-speech">Verb</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">to be<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example">わしはわし<b>じゃ</b>。</li><li class="romanji-example">Washi wa washi <b>ja</b>.</li><li class="english-example">I <b>am</b> me.</li></ul></li></ul><div class="part-of-speech">Noun</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">generic name for a large snake; a heavy drinker</li><li class="definition-entry">a moral wrong; a bad attitude; maliciousness, wickedness; such a person</li><li class="definition-entry">short for 麝香 (jakō, “musk”); short for 麝香鹿 (jakōjika, “musk deer”)</li></ul>		じゃ		Variation on the Japanese copula だ (da) used in western Japan, such as in Chūgoku, Shikoku, or Kyūshū; particularly common in Hiroshima. The use of じゃ (ja) is also common in fictional dialogue attributed to archaic characters. In Kansai, じゃ (ja) changed to や (ya) in the late Edo period.

暖かい	あたたかい	暖かい	<div class="part-of-speech">Adjective</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">physically warm</li><li class="definition-entry">financially well off, rich</li><li class="definition-entry">thoughtful, understanding, emotionally warm</li></ul>	a̠ta̠ta̠ka̠i	あたた<span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>か</span>い	<ruby>暖<rt><span class="normal-rt">あたた</span></rt></ruby><span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>か</span>いい	Depending on the author, the different kanji spellings might be used to distinguish specific nuances of meaning.  暖かい (atatakai) may emphasize "surrounding warmth", primarily the temperature of the weather, but also a warm and cozy sweater or futon (as opposed to one that is physically hot, such as from the dryer or heated by an electric blanket).  By contrast, 温かい (atatakai) emphasizes the temperature of objects or the subjective impression of feelings.

パン	パン		<div class="part-of-speech">Noun</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">bread, pastries, any baked good with a crust (a type of food)</li><li class="definition-entry">(cooking) a pan (flat vessel used for cooking)</li><li class="definition-entry">(cinematography) a pan shot (a film shot where the camera is turned horizontally)</li></ul>		パン		The kanji spellings 麺麭, 麺包, and 麪包 are examples of jukujikun. Use of these spellings is extremely rare in modern Japanese.<br>While usually translated as 'bread', the term also covers a wide variety of baked goods that would not be called bread in English. This includes bread-like sweets like brioche, filled puff pastries and similar items, as well as various Asian steamed dough dumplings.

一	いち	一	<div class="part-of-speech">Numeral</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">one, 1</li></ul>	it͡ɕi	い<span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>ち</span>	<ruby>一<rt><span class="normal-rt">い</span><span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>ち</span></rt></ruby>	一 is always omitted before 十 (jū, “ten”) and 百 (hyaku, “hundred”), but never before the "myriad scale digit names" including 万 (man, “ten thousand”), 億 (oku, “hundred million”), 兆 (chō, “trillion”) and their combinations.<br>When before 千 (sen, “thousand”), 一 is omitted when 千 is not directly followed by 万, i.e. not before 千万 (senman, “ten million”), and the omission is optional.<br>千百十一 (senhyakujūichi, “1,111”)<br>百万 (hyakuman, “1,000,000”)<br>一千万 (issenman, “10,000,000”)<br>千一万 (sen'ichiman, “10,010,000”)

この	この		<div class="part-of-speech">Adnominal</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">(deictically) this ... (near the speaker)<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><b>この</b><ruby>車<rt>くるま</rt></ruby>は<ruby>緑<rt>みどり</rt></ruby>です。</li><li class="romanji-example"><b>Kono</b> kuruma wa midori desu.</li><li class="english-example"><b>This</b> car is green.</li></ul></li><li class="definition-entry">(anaphorically) this ... I am talking about (only the speaker knows)</li><li class="definition-entry">used before words for insults to add emphasis; compare Chinese 這／这 (zhè, “this”) and Vietnamese này (“this”)<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><b>この</b><ruby>馬<rt>ば</rt></ruby><ruby>鹿<rt>か</rt></ruby>！</li><li class="romanji-example"><b>Kono</b> baka!</li><li class="english-example"><b>You</b> idiot!</li></ul></li><li class="definition-entry">I, me: used before one's own name to refer to oneself arrogantly</li></ul>	ko̞no̞	この		This term can only be used when followed by a noun or noun phrase. It cannot be used as a standalone pronoun. Examples:<br>Correct:<br>この色が好きです。 ― Kono iro ga suki desu. ― I like this color.<br>A noun follows kono.<br>Incorrect:<br>このが好きです。 ― Kono ga suki desu. ― I like this ...<br>There must be a noun in place of the "...", as this is the adnominal, or attributive form, incapable of standing independently.<br>Using kono in this way is comparable to using English the without a noun after it (*I like the.).<br>Correct:<br>これが好きです。 ― Kore ga suki desu. ― I like this.<br>A pronoun is used in the stead of the attributive, resulting in the desired meaning of 'this', with no verbal reference present nor required as to the identity of the object.<br>Almost always spelled in hiragana as この.

もう	もう		<div class="part-of-speech">Adverb</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">already (having reached a certain situation or finished a certain action)</li><li class="definition-entry">already the time to do so; shortly, soon<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example">９<ruby>時<rt>じ</rt></ruby>ですから<b>もう</b><ruby>失<rt>しつ</rt></ruby><ruby>礼<rt>れい</rt></ruby>します。</li><li class="romanji-example">Ku-ji desu kara <b>mō</b> shitsurei shimasu.</li><li class="english-example">It's nine o'clock so I must leave <b>shortly</b>.</li></ul></li><li class="definition-entry">more, further<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><b>もう</b><ruby>一<rt>いっ</rt></ruby><ruby>杯<rt>ぱい</rt></ruby>ください。</li><li class="romanji-example"><b>Mō</b> ippai kudasai.</li><li class="english-example">Give me one <b>more</b> glass.</li></ul></li><li class="definition-entry">(chiefly in the negative) any more, again<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><ruby>私<rt>わたし</rt></ruby>は<b>もう</b><ruby>歩<rt>ある</rt></ruby>けない。</li><li class="romanji-example">Watashi wa <b>mō</b> arukenai.</li><li class="english-example">I can't walk <b>any more</b>.</li></ul><ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><b>もう</b>しませんから<ruby>許<rt>ゆる</rt></ruby>してください。</li><li class="romanji-example"><b>Mō</b> shimasen kara yurushite kudasai.</li><li class="english-example">Please forgive me, I won't do it <b>again</b>.</li></ul></li></ul>	mo̞ː	<span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>も</span>う		A common mistake among Japanese language learners is to use もう as a general term meaning more; the correct term in many cases is もっと (motto).
欲しい	ほしい	欲しい	<div class="part-of-speech">Adjective</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">wanted, desired, needed<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><ruby>私<rt>わたし</rt></ruby>はこの<ruby>本<rt>ほん</rt></ruby>が<b><ruby>欲<rt>ほ</rt></ruby>しい</b>です。</li><li class="romanji-example">Watashi wa kono hon ga <b>hoshii</b> desu.</li><li class="english-example">As for me, this book is <b>wanted</b> → I <b>want</b> this book.</li></ul></li><li class="definition-entry">(after a -て verb form) wanted or desirable that someone perform the verb<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><ruby>彼<rt>かの</rt></ruby><ruby>女<rt>じょ</rt></ruby>に<ruby>勝<rt>か</rt></ruby>って<b>ほしかった</b>。</li><li class="romanji-example">Kanojo ni katte <b>hoshikatta</b>.</li><li class="english-example">It was <b>wanted</b> of her to win → I <b>wanted</b> her to win.</li></ul></li></ul>	ho̞ɕiː	ほ<span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>し</span>い	<ruby>欲<rt><span class="normal-rt">ほ</span></rt></ruby><span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>し</span>いい	About ほしい used to describe nouns:<br>Unlike English want, Japanese 欲しい is an adjective modifying the thing desired or wanted.<br>About -て + ほしい:<br>The agent of the verb can be introduced by either に (ni) or が (ga).<br>娘に笑ってほしい。Musume ni waratte hoshii.I want my daughter to have a happy face.<br>雨が降ってほしい。Ame ga futte hoshii.(Lit. "The rain falls and (it) is wanted") I am waiting for a rain.<br>に can be ambiguous when an indirect object is involved, as this also takes に.<br>佐藤さんに英語を教えてほしい。Satō-san ni Eigo o oshiete hoshii.I want someone to teach Sato English. (indirect object)I want Sato to teach English. (agent)<br>When the subject (the person that wants) is not the speaker, it is introduced by the topic maker は (wa).<br>親は私にもっとしっかりしてほしい。Oya wa watashi ni motto shikkari shite hoshii.My parents want me to work harder.<br>ほしい does not express the meaning "someone wants to do something themselves". In that case, the verb suffix たい is usually used instead. Compare:<br>彼女は帰りたいと言った。Kanojo wa kaeritai to itta.She said she wants to go home.<br>彼女は帰ってほしい。Kanojo wa kaette hoshii.I want her to go home.<br>A related verb denoting willingness (欲する (hossuru)) exists, but its usage is quite uncommon.<br>Depending on context, the usage of this word could be considered overly direct or childish.<br>When following the ない (nai) form of a verb + で (de), this construction means "don't want [someone else] to".<br>君は帰らないでほしい。Kimi wa kaeranaide hoshii.I don't want you to go back.<br>This term is commonly spelled in hiragana, with the kanji spelling reserved for formal contexts.

ください	ください		<div class="part-of-speech">Verb</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">(polite) Imperative of 下さる</li></ul>		ください		Nowadays, except in very formal or traditional contexts, this term is normally written in kana. Outside of such contexts, the spelling 「下さい」 can emphasize the meaning of "give" associated with 「下」, though this is rare.<br>The imperative sense is either attached to the て form (te-form) of a verb, or a noun prefixed with お (o-) or ご (go-).<br>ちょっとお待ちください。Chotto o-machi kudasai.Please wait a little. (Here 待ち is the continuative form of 待つ.)<br>これをご覧ください。Kore o go-ran kudasai.Please have a look at this.

漢字	かんじ	漢字	<div class="part-of-speech">Noun</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">kanji</li></ul>	kã̠ɲ̟d͡ʑi	かんじ	<ruby>漢<rt><span class="normal-rt">か</span><span class="normal-rt">ん</span></rt></ruby><ruby>字<rt><span class="normal-rt">じ</span></rt></ruby>	The term literally means “Chinese characters”, and refers broadly to any such ideographic or logographic character originating in written Chinese, or created anew along similar lines. This latter category includes some characters created in Japan from originally Chinese elements and called 国字 (kokuji, literally “national (i.e. Japanese) characters”), and other characters that were modified over time into distinctly Japanese forms and called 新字体 (shinjitai, literally “new character forms”). Contrast with 仮名 (kana, literally “borrowed label”), uniquely Japanese phonetic characters derived either from cursive forms of kanji (the 平仮名 (hiragana)) or from shorthand that abbreviated characters to use just specific parts of the original kanji (the 片仮名 (katakana)).

feelings. トイレ	トイレ		<div class="part-of-speech">Noun</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">bathroom, restroom, lavatory</li><li class="definition-entry">toilet</li></ul>	to̞iɾe̞	<span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>ト</span>イレ		While previously this term was considered rather crude when used to refer to the bathroom (being literally “toilet”), its usage is increasingly accepted, particularly in the younger generation – 「トイレはどこですか。」 (“Where is the toilet?”) is generally acceptable. ある	ある		<div class="part-of-speech">Verb</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">to exist, to be, to have</li><li class="definition-entry">(of a matter or state) to be recognized, be acknowledged<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><ruby>近<rt>きん</rt></ruby><ruby>年<rt>ねん</rt></ruby>、<ruby>物<rt>ぶっ</rt></ruby><ruby>価<rt>か</rt></ruby>の<ruby>上<rt>じょう</rt></ruby><ruby>昇<rt>しょう</rt></ruby>に<b>ある</b>。</li><li depend on, lie in, be influenced by<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><ruby>成<rt>せい</rt></ruby><ruby>功<rt>こう</rt></ruby>の<ruby>秘<rt>ひ</rt></ruby><ruby>訣<rt>けつ</rt></ruby>は<ruby>努<rt>ど</rt></ruby><ruby>力<rt>りょく</rt></ruby>に<b>ある</b>。</li><li class="romanji-example">Seikō no hiketsu wa doryoku ni <b>aru</b>.</li><li class="english-example">The secret to success <b>lies</b> in hard work.</li></ul></li><li class="definition-entry">to be alive, surviving<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><ruby>世<rt>よ</rt></ruby>に<b>ある</b><ruby>間<rt>あいだ</rt></ruby></li><li class="romanji-example">yo ni <b>aru</b> aida</li><li class="english-example">during one's lifetime</li></ul></li><li class="definition-entry">to have</li><li class="definition-entry">(auxiliary, after the conjunctive て (te) form of a transitive verb)</li><li class="definition-entry">(auxiliary, after the 連用形 (ren'yōkei, continuative form) of a verb followed by つつ (tsutsu)) Indicating that the action or effect is currently ongoing.<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><ruby>彼女<rt>かのじょ</rt></ruby>の<ruby>体<rt>たい</rt></ruby><ruby>調<rt>ちょう</rt></ruby>は<ruby>徐<rt>じょ</rt></ruby>々に<ruby>回<rt>かい</rt></ruby><ruby>復<rt>ふく</rt></ruby>しつつ<b>ある</b>。</li><li class="romanji-example">Kanojo no taichō wa jojoni kaifuku shitsutsu <b>aru</b>.</li><li class="english-example">Her health condition <b>is</b> gradually improving.</li></ul></li><li class="definition-entry">(auxiliary, after a noun followed by で (de)) Judging that something has a certain nature or state based on its explanation; or simply expressing assertion (see: である (de aru))<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example">ロバは<ruby>馬<rt>うま</rt></ruby>と<ruby>同<rt>どう</rt></ruby><ruby>族<rt>ぞく</rt></ruby>で<b>ある</b>。</li><li class="romanji-example">Roba wa uma to dōzoku de <b>aru</b>.</li><li class="english-example">The donkey <b>belongs</b> to the same family as the horse.</li></ul><ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example">この<ruby>仕<rt>し</rt></ruby><ruby>事<rt>ごと</rt></ruby>は<ruby>簡<rt>かん</rt></ruby><ruby>単<rt>たん</rt></ruby>では<b>ある</b>が、<ruby>単<rt>たん</rt></ruby><ruby>調<rt>ちょう</rt></ruby>だ。</li><li class="romanji-example">Kono shigoto wa kantan de wa <b>aru</b> ga, tanchō da.</li><li class="english-example">Easy as the job <b>is</b>, it's monotonous.</li></ul></li><li class="definition-entry">(auxiliary, after the 連用形 (ren'yōkei, continuative form) of a verb or a Sino-Japanese action noun which is usually prefixed with お (o) or ご (go) respectively) Expressing respect toward the person performing the action<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example">おいで<b>あれ</b>。</li><li class="romanji-example">O-ide <b>are</b>.</li><li class="english-example">Please come.</li></ul><ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example">ご<ruby>理<rt>り</rt></ruby><ruby>解<rt>かい</rt></ruby><b>ある</b>とありがたいです。</li><li class="romanji-example">Go-rikai <b>aru</b> to arigatai desu.</li><li class="english-example">It would be greatly appreciated if there <b>is</b> understanding (from you).</li></ul></li></ul><div class="part-of-speech">Adnominal</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">certain, one, some<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><b>ある</b><ruby>政<rt>せい</rt></ruby><ruby>治<rt>じ</rt></ruby><ruby>家<rt>か</rt></ruby>がそう<ruby>言<rt>い</rt></ruby>った。</li><li class="romanji-example"><b>Aru</b> seijika ga sō itta.</li><li class="english-example">A <b>certain</b> politician said so.</li></ul></li></ul>	a̠ɾɯ̟	<span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>あ</span>る		Various combinations with the term have certain meanings:<br>Used after と (to, quotation particle), meaning “to say; to give information”, similar to という (to iu).<br>メモには午後二時に来社するとある。Memo ni wa gogo ni-ji ni raisha suru to aru.On the memo it says "Come to the company at 2 p.m."<br>Used in the form とあって (to atte), meaning “due to (the situation or result)”, which is more formal than だから (dakara).<br>このイベントは、無料で参加できるとあって、多くの人が集まった。Kono ibento wa, muryō de sanka dekiru to atte, ōku no hito ga atsumatta.Many people gathered for this event due to the fact that it was free to participate.<br>Used in the form だけある (dake aru) or だけのことはある (dake no koto wa aru), expressing that a suitable state or result can be obtained, which roughly translates as “as expected; it is no surprise”.<br>彼女は有名大学を出ているだけあって、とても頭がいい。Kanojo wa yūmei daigaku o dete iru dake atte, totemo atama ga ii.As expected from someone who graduated from a famous university, she is very intelligent.<br>Used in the form ことがある (koto ga aru), indicating a possible reaction to or result from a situation, or referring to experience, literally “There exists that ...”.<br>練習すれば上達することがある。Renshū sureba jōtatsu suru koto ga aru.If you practice, you may improve.<br>日本に行ったことがありますか。Nihon ni itta koto ga arimasu ka.Have you ever been to Japan?<br>Used in the form にあって (ni atte), meaning “in terms of; within the context of”.<br>世界的なパンデミックにあって、人々はマスクや手洗いをすることが重要です。Sekai-teki na pandemikku ni atte, hitobito wa masuku ya tearai o suru koto ga jūyō desu.During the global pandemic, it is important for people to wear masks and wash their hands.<br>Used in the form にしてある (ni shite aru), indicating that someone is intentionally considering something as if it were a certain way, even if it is not actually so.<br>彼女は結婚していないが、既に結婚した気分にしてある。Kanojo wa kekkon shite inai ga, sudeni kekkon shita kibun ni shite aru.She's not married yet, but she already feels as if she were.

する	する		<div class="part-of-speech">Verb</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">(intransitive)</li><li class="definition-entry">(transitive)</li><li class="definition-entry">(auxiliary)</li><li class="definition-entry">to print something (from the way the paper would be placed on the printing block and <b>rubbed</b>)</li><li class="definition-entry">to pick someone's pocket (possibly from the way a pickpocket must <b>slide</b> along unnoticed; compare English slick)</li><li class="definition-entry">to slide, to rub, to chafe, to strike (as in a match, by rubbing); to lose or waste money</li><li class="definition-entry">irregular reading for 剃る (soru, “to shave”)</li></ul>	sɨɾɯ̟	する		The verb する (“to do”) is seldom written in kanji (為る).<br>It is common to use する after certain nouns to indicate that the noun is being done; this is highly productive, meaning many nouns can be used as verbs in this way. Some examples are:<br>勉強 (benkyō, “studying”) → 勉強する (benkyō suru, “to do studying” → “to study”)<br>旅行 (ryokō, “journey”) → 旅行する (ryokō suru, “to do journey” → “to travel”)<br>アップロード (appurōdo, “upload”) → アップロードする (appurōdo suru, “to do uploading” → “to upload”)

七つ	ななつ	七つ	<div class="part-of-speech">Noun</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">seven things<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><b><ruby>七<rt>なな</rt></ruby>つ</b>の<ruby>美徳<rt>びとく</rt></ruby></li><li class="romanji-example"><b>nanatsu</b> no bitoku</li><li class="english-example">seven virtues</li></ul><ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><b><ruby>七<rt>なな</rt></ruby>つ</b>の<ruby>大罪<rt>だいざい</rt></ruby></li><li class="romanji-example"><b>nanatsu</b> no daizai</li><li class="english-example">seven deadly sins</li></ul><ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><b><ruby>七<rt>なな</rt></ruby>つ</b>の<ruby>小人<rt>こびと</rt></ruby></li><li class="romanji-example"><b>nanatsu</b> no kobito</li><li class="english-example">the Seven Dwarfs</li></ul></li></ul>	na̠na̠t͡sɨ	な<span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>な</span>つ	<ruby>七<rt><span class="normal-rt">な</span><span class="pitch-container"><span class="pitch-mark">•</span>な</span></rt></ruby>つ	七つ (nanatsu) is used when the items counted have no specific counter category. 要る	いる	要る	<div class="part-of-speech">Verb</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">to be necessary, to need<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example">お<ruby>金<rt>かね</rt></ruby>が<b><ruby>要<rt>い</rt></ruby>ります</b>か？—いいえ、<b><ruby>要<rt>い</rt></ruby>りません</b>。</li><li class="romanji-example">Okane ga <b>irimasu</b> ka? — Īe, <b>irimasen</b>.</li><li class="english-example">Do you <b>need</b> money? — No, I <b>don't need</b> money.</li></ul><ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example">この<ruby>本<rt>ほん</rt></ruby>は<b><ruby>要<rt>い</rt></ruby>ります</b>か？</li><li class="romanji-example">Kono hon wa <b>irimasu</b> ka?</li><li class="english-example">Do you <b>need</b> this book?</li></ul></li></ul>	iɾɯ̟	いる	<ruby>要<rt><span class="normal-rt">い</span></rt></ruby>る	Usually spelled in hiragana, with the kanji being used for formal contexts. The past form is falling out of use.[5]
その	その		<div class="part-of-speech">Adnominal</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">(deictically) that ... near you (near the addressee)<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><ruby>私<rt>わたし</rt></ruby>が<ruby>読<rt>よ</rt></ruby>みたいのは、<b>その</b><ruby>本<rt>ほん</rt></ruby>です。</li><li class="romanji-example">Watashi ga yomitai no wa, <b>sono</b> hon desu.</li><li class="english-example">What I want to read is <b>that</b> book.</li></ul></li><li class="definition-entry">(anaphorically) the ... we are talking about.</li><li class="definition-entry">...of it; its; their<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><b>その</b>お<ruby>姉<rt>ねえ</rt></ruby>さん</li><li class="romanji-example"><b>sono</b> onēsan</li><li class="english-example"><b>her</b> elder sister</li></ul></li><li class="definition-entry">used in a title and before a number to indicate that number of part, chapter, volume, episode, or installment; compare suffixes 編 (-hen), 話 (-wa), 巻 (-kan), 集 (-shū), 章 (-shō), 上 (jō), 中 (chū) and 下 (ge)<ul class="example-sentence"><li class="japanese-example"><ruby>日<rt>に</rt></ruby><ruby>本<rt>ほん</rt></ruby><ruby>映<rt>えい</rt></ruby><ruby>画<rt>が</rt></ruby><ruby>論<rt>ろん</rt></ruby><b>その</b><ruby>三<rt>さん</rt></ruby></li><li class="romanji-example">Nihon Eigaron <b>Sono</b> San</li><li class="english-example">Japanese Films: <b>Part</b> 3</li></ul></li></ul>	so̞no̞	その		Sono is anaphorically used to refer to things and experiences that either the speaker or the listener knows about, but not both. Compare the two following sentences:<br>その本はやすいですか。Sono hon wa yasui desu ka.Is that book (which we are talking about) cheap? (anaphorically)Is that book (I'm pointing to/near you/of yours) cheap? (deictically)<br>あの本はやすいですか。Ano hon wa yasui desu ka.Is the book (which we both know) cheap? (anaphorically)Is that book (over there, which you and I can see) cheap? (deictically)<br>The hiragana spelling その is preferred to the kanji spelling 其の, which is rarely used.

こちら	こちら		<div class="part-of-speech">Pronoun</div><ul class="word-definitions"><li class="definition-entry">this direction, this way</li><li class="definition-entry">this person; I, me, we, us</li><li class="definition-entry">this thing</li></ul>	ko̞t͡ɕiɾa̠	こちら		The alternative kanji spelling is rarely used.<br>The usage as a pronoun is often used in impersonal settings with an indeterminate number of people being referred to. For example, when a single speaker is speaking for his entire company on a phone or in negotiations.
